use project1;
CREATE TABLE Branch (
    BranchID INT AUTO_INCREMENT PRIMARY KEY,
    BranchName VARCHAR(100),
    Location VARCHAR(100),
    ContactNumber VARCHAR(15),
    Email VARCHAR(100),
    INDEX (BranchName)
);

CREATE TABLE Members (
    MemberID INT AUTO_INCREMENT PRIMARY KEY,
    Name VARCHAR(100),
    ContactNumber VARCHAR(15),
    Email VARCHAR(100),
    MembershipType VARCHAR(50),
    BranchID INT,
    FOREIGN KEY (BranchID) REFERENCES Branch(BranchID)
);

CREATE TABLE Staff (
    StaffID INT AUTO_INCREMENT PRIMARY KEY,
    Name VARCHAR(100),
    Designation VARCHAR(100),
    ContactNumber VARCHAR(15),
    Email VARCHAR(100)
);


CREATE TABLE Events (
    EventID INT AUTO_INCREMENT PRIMARY KEY,
    EventName VARCHAR(100),
    Date DATE,
    BranchID INT ,FOREIGN KEY (BranchID) REFERENCES Branch(BranchID)
);

CREATE TABLE Facilities (
    FacilityID INT AUTO_INCREMENT PRIMARY KEY,
    FacilityName VARCHAR(100),
    Availability BOOLEAN
);

CREATE TABLE Payments (
    PaymentID INT AUTO_INCREMENT PRIMARY KEY,
    MemberID INT,
    Amount DECIMAL(10,2),
    PaymentDate DATE,
    FOREIGN KEY (MemberID) REFERENCES Members(MemberID)
);

16/02/24, 15:24 - Sabhya: abstract class payment {
	int cno;
	String cholder;
	double camt;
	abstract void procpaym();
	abstract void dispdet();
}
class credcard extends payment {
	int expiry;
	credcard(int x, String y, double z, int a) {
		super.cno = x;
		super.cholder = y;
		super.camt = z;
		expiry = a;
	}
	void procpaym() {
		System.out.println("Credit card payment in process.");
	}
	void dispdet() {
		System.out.println("Card number: "+super.cno+", Card Holder: "+super.cholder+", Days to expiry: "+expiry+
					", Amount: "+super.camt);
	}
}
class debcard extends payment {
	int pin;
	debcard(int x, String y, double z, int a) {
		super.cno = x;
		super.cholder = y;
		super.camt = z;
		pin = a;
	}
	void procpaym() {
		System.out.println("Debit card payment in process.");
	}
	void dispdet() {
		System.out.println("Card number: "+super.cno+", Card Holder: "+super.cholder+", Pin: "+pin+
					", Amount: "+super.camt);
	}
}
public class Exp07 {
	public static void main(String[] args) {
		payment cred = new credcard(99999999, "Slowni", 999999, 1000);
		payment deb = new debcard(696969, "Kalash", 25, 1234);
		cred.procpaym();
		cred.dispdet();
		deb.procpaym();
		deb.dispdet();
	}
}

20/03/24, 23:10 - Dumbloni Sembla: Branch:

Attributes:
BranchID (Primary Key)
BranchName
Location
ContactNumber
Email
Functional Dependencies:
BranchID -> BranchName, Location, ContactNumber, Email
Normal Forms:
First Normal Form (1NF): All attributes are atomic.
Second Normal Form (2NF): No partial dependencies exist.
Third Normal Form (3NF): No transitive dependencies exist.
Conclusion: The Branch table is already in 3NF.
Members:

Attributes:
MemberID (Primary Key)
Name
ContactNumber
Email
MembershipType
BranchID (Foreign Key)
Functional Dependencies:
MemberID -> Name, ContactNumber, Email, MembershipType, BranchID
Normal Forms:
First Normal Form (1NF): All attributes are atomic.
Second Normal Form (2NF): No partial dependencies exist.
Third Normal Form (3NF): No transitive dependencies exist.
Conclusion: The Members table is already in 3NF.
Staff:

Attributes:
StaffID (Primary Key)
Name
Designation
ContactNumber
Email
Functional Dependencies:
StaffID -> Name, Designation, ContactNumber, Email
Normal Forms:
First Normal Form (1NF): All attributes are atomic.
Second Normal Form (2NF): No partial dependencies exist.
Third Normal Form (3NF): No transitive dependencies exist.
Conclusion: The Staff table is already in 3NF.
Events:

Attributes:
EventID (Primary Key)
EventName
Date
BranchID (Foreign Key)
Functional Dependencies:
EventID -> EventName, Date, BranchID
Normal Forms:
First Normal Form (1NF): All attributes are atomic.
Second Normal Form (2NF): No partial dependencies exist.
Third Normal Form (3NF): No transitive dependencies exist.
Conclusion: The Events table is already in 3NF.
Facilities:

Attributes:
FacilityID (Primary Key)
FacilityName
Availability
Functional Dependencies:
FacilityID -> FacilityName, Availability
Normal Forms:
First Normal Form (1NF): All attributes are atomic.
Second Normal Form (2NF): No partial dependencies exist.
Third Normal Form (3NF): No transitive dependencies exist.
Conclusion: The Facilities table is already in 3NF.
Payments:

Attributes:

Amount
PaymentDate
Functional Dependencies:
PaymentID -> Amount, PaymentDate
Normal Forms:
First Normal Form (1NF): All attributes are atomic.
Second Normal Form (2NF): No partial dependencies exist.
Third Normal Form (3NF): No transitive dependencies exist.
Conclusion: The Payments table is already in 3NF.

02/04/24, 17:52 - Dumbloni Sembla: const express = require('express');

const bodyParser = require('body-parser');
const mysql = require('mysql2');
const app = express();

// MySQL database connection
const db = mysql.createConnection({
    host: 'localhost',
    user: 'root',
    password: 'root',
    database: 'license'
});

db.connect((err) => {
    if (err) {
        throw err;
    }
    console.log('Connected to MySQL database');
});

// Body parser middleware
app.use(bodyParser.json());
app.use(bodyParser.urlencoded({ extended: false }));

// Handle form submission
app.post('/submitForm', (req, res) => {
    const formData = req.body;

    // Insert data into MySQL database
    const sql = `INSERT INTO license_applications (fullname, fathername, dob, address, pincode, nationality, contact, licensetype)
                 VALUES (?, ?, ?, ?, ?, ?, ?, ?)`;
    const values = [
        formData.fullname,
        formData.fathername,
        formData.dob,
        formData.address,
        formData.pincode,
        formData.nationality,
        formData.contact,
        formData.licensetype
    ];

    db.query(sql, values, (err, result) => {
        if (err) {
            console.error('Error inserting data:', err);
            res.status(500).send('Error submitting application');
        } else {
            console.log('Application submitted successfully');
            res.status(200).send('Application submitted successfully');
        }
    });
});

const PORT = process.env.PORT || 3030;
app.listen(PORT, () => {
    console.log(`Server running on port ${PORT}`);
});

<script>
    document.getElementById('licenseForm').addEventListener('submit', function(event) {
        event.preventDefault(); // Prevent the default form submission

        // Fetch the form data
        const formData = new FormData(this);

        // Send the form data to the server using fetch API
        fetch('/submitForm', {
            method: 'POST',
            body: formData
        })
        .then(response => {
            if (response.ok) {
                return response.text(); // Return response text if successful
            }
            throw new Error('Network response was not ok.');
        })
        .then(data => {
            // Display success message or perform any other actions
            alert(data);
        })
        .catch(error => {
            // Handle errors
            console.error('Error:', error);
            alert('Error submitting form. Please try again.');
        });
    });
</script>

02/04/24, 18:36 - Dumbloni Sembla: const express = require('express');
const bodyParser = require('body-parser');
const mysql = require('mysql2');
const app = express();

// Function to establish MySQL database connection
function connectToDatabase() {
    return mysql.createConnection({
        host: 'localhost',
        user: 'root',
        password: 'root',
        database: 'license'
    });
}

// MySQL database connection
const db = connectToDatabase();

db.connect((err) => {
    if (err) {
        throw err;
    }
    console.log('Connected to MySQL database');
});

// Body parser middleware
app.use(bodyParser.json());
app.use(bodyParser.urlencoded({ extended: false }));

// Handle form submission (POST request)
app.post('/submitForm', (req, res) => {
    const formData = req.body;

    // Insert data into MySQL database
    const sql = `INSERT INTO license_applications (fullname, fathername, dob, address, pincode, nationality, contact, licensetype)
                 VALUES (?, ?, ?, ?, ?, ?, ?, ?)`;
    const values = [
        formData.fullname,
        formData.fathername,
        formData.dob,
        formData.address,
        formData.pincode,
        formData.nationality,
        formData.contact,
        formData.licensetype
    ];

    app.get('/', (req, res) => {
        res.sendFile(__dirname + '/index.html');
      });
      
    db.query(sql, values, (err, result) => {
        if (err) {
            console.error('Error inserting data:', err);
            res.status(500).send('Error submitting application');
        } else {
            console.log('Application submitted successfully');
            res.status(200).send('Application submitted successfully');
        }
    });
});

const PORT = process.env.PORT || 9999;
app.listen(PORT, () => {
    console.log(`Server running on port ${PORT}`);
});

17/10/24, 20:39 - Sabhya: Introduction to Greyscale Morphology (in context to the presentation)
Greyscale Morphology is an essential tool in the field of image and video processing, extending the concepts of binary morphology (typically applied to binary images) to greyscale images. Morphological operations modify the structure of images based on their intensity values and are particularly effective for analyzing shapes and structures in complex environments.

What is Morphology?
In general, morphology refers to the study of shapes and structures. In image processing, morphological techniques focus on the geometric structure of objects within an image. Traditional morphology operates on binary images, where pixels are either black (0) or white (1), and processes them based on the arrangement of these pixel values.

Why Greyscale Morphology?
When working with greyscale images, which contain varying intensities (usually from 0 to 255), the binary approach becomes insufficient. Greyscale Morphology applies the same morphological operations‚Äîsuch as erosion, dilation, opening, and closing‚Äîbut instead of operating on binary values, these operations are applied to the intensity levels of the greyscale pixels.

Greyscale morphology is particularly useful for:

Noise reduction: It can eliminate unwanted small regions or enhance image features.
Edge detection: Enhancing the boundaries of objects in an image.
Object segmentation: Separating different objects within an image by analyzing their structure.
Key Operations in Greyscale Morphology
Greyscale morphology relies on a structuring element (a small matrix) that is moved over the image and interacts with pixel values in a localized way. The key operations in greyscale morphology are:

Erosion: Erosion in greyscale reduces the brightness of objects. It darkens the brighter areas by sliding the structuring element across the image and replacing each pixel with the minimum value under the structuring element.

Use case: Removing small bright spots from an image or separating close objects.
Dilation: Dilation in greyscale increases the brightness of objects. It brightens the darker regions by replacing each pixel with the maximum value under the structuring element.

Use case: Filling small dark holes or gaps within an image.
Opening: This operation is a combination of erosion followed by dilation. It removes small bright spots or noise while preserving the shape and size of the larger objects.

Use case: Noise reduction while keeping object boundaries intact.
Closing: The reverse of opening, this operation applies dilation followed by erosion. It is useful for closing small gaps within objects.

Use case: Filling small holes or gaps within the object structure.
Structuring Elements and their Impact
A key factor in all morphological operations is the structuring element (SE). The structuring element defines the neighborhood of pixels that the morphological operation will analyze. Common shapes include:

Square or rectangular: Applies uniformly across regions.
Elliptical or circular: Preserves more rounded features, making it ideal for processing curved edges.
The choice of structuring element significantly impacts the result of morphological operations, as different shapes and sizes of SE will affect how much of the image is modified.

Applications of Greyscale Morphology
Greyscale morphology finds wide applications in several domains:

Medical Imaging: Used for noise reduction in MRI scans, or to enhance specific features like blood vessels or tumors.
Satellite Image Processing: Removing noise from satellite imagery or enhancing the contrast between land and water.
Video Processing: Enhancing frames in video sequences by cleaning up noise and enhancing object boundaries frame by frame.
In summary, greyscale morphology allows for powerful transformations of images by focusing on intensity variations and structural relationships within the image. In this presentation, we will explore how these techniques can be implemented, their applications, and how they can be adapted for video processing as well. We‚Äôll also demonstrate these operations using practical code examples, making the concept hands-on and easier to understand.

With this understanding, we can dive deeper into individual morphological operations and their impact on real-world images, as covered in the next sections of this presentation.

17/10/24, 20:45 - Sabhya: Erosion
Replaces each pixel with the minimum value in its neighborhood.
Effect:
Reduces the size of bright regions.
Removes small noise or details.
Visual Example:
Show an original greyscale image and the result after applying erosion.
Highlight areas where small details are removed.
Dilation
Replaces each pixel with the maximum value in its neighborhood.
Effect:
Expands bright regions.
Fills in small holes and connects nearby features.
Visual Example:
Display the original image alongside the dilated version.
Use arrows or highlights to indicate areas where objects have expanded.
Opening
Sequence of erosion followed by dilation.
Effect:
Removes small objects and noise.
Preserves the shape and size of larger objects.
Visual Example:
Show a comparison of the original image, followed by images after erosion and dilation.
Emphasize how small noise is eliminated while larger structures remain intact.
Closing
Sequence of dilation followed by erosion.
Effect:
Closes small gaps or holes in objects.
Smooths object boundaries.
Visual Example:
Present the original image and the result after closing.
Highlight how gaps in objects are filled.

17/10/24, 21:12 - Sabhya: 2. Satellite Image Processing
Application: Analyzing and interpreting satellite images for land use, vegetation cover, and environmental monitoring.
Expansion: Greyscale morphology helps in the removal of atmospheric noise and highlights significant land features. Dilation can enhance the visibility of water bodies, while erosion can be used to eliminate small clouds or artifacts, making the analysis of geographical and environmental changes more accurate.
3. Object Detection and Tracking
Application: Identifying and tracking objects in video sequences or still images.
Expansion: Greyscale morphology is used to preprocess images to enhance features of interest before applying more complex algorithms. For instance, opening can remove irrelevant small objects, allowing algorithms to focus on larger, more significant features for tracking in video surveillance or autonomous vehicles.
4. Image Segmentation
Application: Separating different regions or objects within an image for analysis.
Expansion: In applications like automated quality control in manufacturing or document analysis, greyscale morphology can segment images based on intensity levels. Dilation can be used to expand regions of interest, while erosion helps refine boundaries, leading to more precise segmentation outcomes.
5. Noise Reduction
Application: Cleaning up images by removing unwanted artifacts or noise.
Expansion: Greyscale morphology is effective in reducing noise in various imaging applications, such as photography or remote sensing. By applying opening to eliminate small noise points and closing to smooth object edges, overall image quality improves, making further analysis more reliable.
6. Edge Detection
Application: Identifying the boundaries of objects within an image.
Expansion: Greyscale morphology can enhance edges by using morphological gradients, which highlight differences between dilated and eroded images. This approach is useful in various fields, including computer vision and robotics, where precise edge detection is crucial for tasks like navigation and object manipulation.
7. Text Recognition
Application: Improving the accuracy of optical character recognition (OCR) systems.
Expansion: In OCR applications, greyscale morphology helps preprocess documents by enhancing text clarity. For instance, closing operations can fill gaps in characters, while opening can help separate closely spaced letters, leading to better recognition rates and more accurate data extraction.

30/03/25, 13:41 - Dumbloni Sembla: ‚úÖ 3. Compare Against Other Distinguishers
üìå Current Limitation in the Paper:

The paper compares SymSum to ZeroSum, an older distinguisher.

However, modern cryptanalysis has developed other distinguishers, such as:

Integral attacks

Differential-linear attacks

Boomerang attacks

The authors do not discuss how SymSum compares to these techniques in terms of efficiency and power.

üìå Why This is Important:

If SymSum is not actually better than other distinguishers, its significance is lower.

Other techniques might already extend beyond 9 rounds, making SymSum less impactful.

Comparing against multiple methods would help place SymSum in the broader field of SHA3 cryptanalysis.

üìå How This Could Be Done:

Run multiple distinguishers on SHA3 and compare results:
‚úÖ How many rounds each method can break?
‚úÖ Computational complexity‚Äîis SymSum the most efficient?
‚úÖ Applicability to different hash variants‚Äîdoes SymSum work better than others in certain cases?

If another method already extends beyond 9 rounds, then SymSum might not be the best approach.

üìå Potential Impact:

If SymSum is found to be stronger or more efficient, it gains credibility as an important cryptanalysis tool.

If another method is better, then future research should focus on that method instead.

18/04/25, 11:23 - Sabhya: The design and implementation of distributed file systems have been extensively studied in both academia and industry. Our implementation builds on conceptual frameworks from peer-reviewed research and production systems, with modifications tailored to a simplified client-server paradigm.

The InterPlanetary File System (IPFS), developed by Juan Benet [1], is a peer-to-peer hypermedia protocol designed to make the web faster, safer, and more open. It introduces a decentralized approach to file storage by referencing files through cryptographic hashes. Files in IPFS are immutable, and content is retrieved through a distributed network of nodes. While our system does not utilize content addressing or peer discovery, the underlying goals of decentralization, redundancy, and availability are aligned. Our simplified version substitutes content hashing with folder-based file identification to preserve human readability and reduce cryptographic overhead for a small-scale deployment.

Ganesh et al. [2] provide a thorough treatment of security considerations in structured peer-to-peer overlays. Their work highlights routing protocols like Pastry and Chord and discusses how secure communication and replication are essential for tolerating adversarial attacks and node failures. Although our system lacks structured overlays and is not peer-driven, we replicate files across static server nodes to ensure data is not lost due to a single point of failure. This method, while static, is a simplified embodiment of the replication strategies discussed in structured P2P systems.

Pan et al. [3] conducted a comprehensive survey of distributed file systems, categorizing them into structured and unstructured overlays and analyzing core attributes such as consistency, performance, scalability, and fault tolerance. Their analysis spans traditional systems like NFS and CIFS to large-scale modern architectures like Ceph, HDFS, and GFS. A significant insight from this survey is that many high-performance systems separate metadata from data blocks, often through a master-slave architecture. In our project, the client acts as both a coordinator and initiator of metadata (folder names), while the servers act as passive recipients. We intentionally merge metadata and data storage into the same folder structure to simplify design.

The Hadoop Distributed File System (HDFS) [6], presented by Shvachko et al., employs a NameNode to handle metadata and multiple DataNodes for file chunk storage. It supports partial replication (default 3 copies) and handles node failures with high resilience. While our project does not chunk files or implement dynamic rebalancing, we draw inspiration from its replication principle. Each server stores a complete copy of every uploaded file, mimicking HDFS‚Äôs fault-tolerant design albeit in a na√Øve form.

Google File System (GFS), an internal file system developed by Google, shares conceptual similarities with HDFS and prioritizes performance for large-scale distributed applications. GFS supports automatic recovery and background rebalancing of data. Our system does not yet handle background rebalancing but leaves room for potential expansion by adding health monitoring and redistribution logic.

Commercial storage systems like Amazon S3 and Google Cloud Storage [7] offer managed, scalable, and secure object storage with strong durability guarantees. These systems use techniques like erasure coding, multi-zone replication, and consistent hashing to manage trillions of objects. While our design does not incorporate such sophistication, we reflect similar architectural goals‚Äîredundancy, remote accessibility, and modularity‚Äîin a much simpler form, making it ideal for introductory exploration of distributed systems.

gRPC, the communication backbone of our implementation, is a high-performance, open-source universal RPC framework initially developed by Google [4]. Built on top of HTTP/2, gRPC supports multiplexed streams, flow control, header compression, and bi-directional communication. We use gRPC in conjunction with Protocol Buffers [5], a language-neutral, platform-neutral, extensible way of serializing structured data, to define services like file upload and search. This significantly reduces overhead and provides a robust communication bridge between clients and servers.

In summary, our system amalgamates concepts from structured P2P networks, distributed file systems like HDFS and IPFS, and communication protocols like gRPC. While not as robust or scalable as the systems it references, it serves as a pedagogical tool that prioritizes ease of understanding and modular experimentation over high throughput or fault-tolerant guarantees.

18/04/25, 11:26 - Sabhya: Abstract:
18/04/25, 11:26 - Sabhya: This paper presents the design and implementation of a simplified yet functional peer-to-peer (P2P) file sharing system that leverages file chunking and distributed storage among multiple peer nodes. The system introduces a decentralized approach to file handling, wherein a central server orchestrates the splitting of uploaded files into fixed-size chunks and distributes these chunks across a predefined set of peers over a local area network (LAN). Each peer acts as an autonomous storage node capable of receiving, storing, and serving file fragments, while the main server manages the coordination, chunk routing, and communication logic.

The implementation utilizes Python sockets for low-level network communication, multi-threading for concurrency, and a graphical user interface (GUI) built with Tkinter to facilitate user interaction for file upload and download operations. Unlike traditional centralized systems, this architecture aims to reduce single points of failure, distribute storage load, and simulate the key principles of a P2P network in a controlled and educational environment. While it currently lacks features such as redundancy, fault tolerance, and secure transmission, the prototype successfully demonstrates the feasibility and foundational concepts of decentralized file distribution. This system serves as a basis for further development into more robust and scalable distributed computing applications.
18/04/25, 11:28 - Sabhya: Intro:
18/04/25, 11:28 - Sabhya: As digital content continues to grow in volume and importance, users frequently encounter challenges in managing and storing large files across personal devices. Limited local storage, data redundancy, and concerns over centralized failure points have driven the need for distributed storage solutions. Peer-to-peer (P2P) systems address these issues by decentralizing data responsibilities, allowing users to store, retrieve, and share content collaboratively across a network of interconnected nodes. These systems distribute workload, mitigate bottlenecks, and improve availability‚Äîmaking them increasingly relevant in both academic exploration and real-world applications.

This project presents the design and implementation of a simplified P2P file sharing system that enables remote, distributed storage through file chunking and peer-based delivery. The goal is to provide users with the ability to store files across multiple nodes in a local network, thereby sparing storage on their personal devices and reducing dependence on any single point of failure. When a file is uploaded through the system's GUI, it is received by a central server, split into 1 MB chunks, and distributed evenly among a set of pre-configured peers for storage. These peers act as lightweight servers, each responsible for receiving, storing, and potentially serving one or more file fragments.

The system was developed with a strong educational focus, aiming to build an intuitive understanding of core distributed computing concepts such as file chunking, socket-based communication, and peer node orchestration. Instead of relying on high-level frameworks like gRPC or HTTP APIs, the project deliberately uses Python‚Äôs low-level socket programming to offer hands-on insight into how data is transmitted over TCP/IP networks. A simple but effective GUI using Tkinter allows users to interact with the system for uploading and downloading files.

The project‚Äôs learning objectives included:

Exploring the foundational architecture of P2P networks.

Implementing custom protocols for file chunking and transfer.

Understanding socket communication at the transport layer.

Applying concurrency techniques to handle multiple clients or peers simultaneously.

Ultimately, this system lays the groundwork for more complex distributed architectures by offering a functioning, modular prototype. While simple in design, it demonstrates key principles that can be extended to support real-world use cases like redundant storage, fault tolerance, and dynamic peer discovery.
18/04/25, 11:32 - Sabhya: System Model:
18/04/25, 11:32 - Sabhya: The proposed system follows a semi-decentralized architecture in which a central server acts as a coordinator and splitter, while peer nodes serve as independent storage units for file fragments. The key novelty lies in the use of file chunking to enable distributed storage across a set of predefined peers. The system is built using socket programming in Python and supports multithreaded request handling for both server and peer components. A graphical client interface allows users to upload and initiate downloads in a controlled LAN environment.

3.1 Overview of the P2P Overlay Network
The system comprises three core node types:

A central server (server.py) responsible for receiving files, splitting them into chunks, and distributing them.

A set of peer nodes (e.g., peer2.py), each running a lightweight TCP server to accept and store incoming file chunks.

A client node (peer1.py) providing a GUI for users to upload or download files.

The list of peers is statically defined within the server code:

PEERS = [ ('10.160.65.237', 9090), ('10.160.65.93', 9090), ]

This fixed configuration supports a simple overlay network with manual initialization and no dynamic discovery or scaling. Chunk distribution is performed in a round-robin fashion, ensuring even distribution of data among available peers.

3.2 File Storage and Chunking
Upon upload, the client establishes a TCP connection with the central server and begins transmitting the file. Files are read and sent in binary format using a fixed chunk size of 1 MB:

CHUNK_SIZE = 1024 * 1024

The client performs the upload using:

with open(file_path, "rb") as f: while True: data = f.read(CHUNK_SIZE) if not data: break client_socket.sendall(data)

The server saves the full file temporarily in a folder named 'server_temp'. It then splits the file into 1 MB chunks using a loop inside the split_file() function:

chunk = f.read(CHUNK_SIZE) chunk_name = f"chunk_{index}" chunk_path = os.path.join("chunks", chunk_name)

Each chunk is saved in a directory called 'chunks' and later distributed to a peer using TCP socket communication. For each chunk, the server connects to a peer and transmits the data after a basic handshake:

s.connect(peer) s.send(os.path.basename(chunk_path).encode()) ack = s.recv(1024) if ack.decode() != 'READY': continue

with open(chunk_path, 'rb') as f: while True: data = f.read(CHUNK_SIZE) if not data: break s.sendall(data)

3.3 Peer Node Behavior
Each peer node runs a persistent TCP socket server listening on port 9090. When contacted by the central server, it performs the following operations:

Receives the name of the incoming chunk.

Sends a 'READY' message as acknowledgment.

Receives the chunk data and writes it to a directory named 'received_chunks'.

This is handled using the following logic:

chunk_name = conn.recv(1024).decode().strip() conn.send(b'READY')

full_path = os.path.join(SAVE_DIR, chunk_name) with open(full_path, 'wb') as f: while True: data = conn.recv(4096) if not data: break f.write(data)

This minimalist behavior allows each peer to act independently, focusing solely on chunk reception and storage.

3.4 Metadata Handling
Currently, metadata is handled implicitly. There is no separate metadata registry, database, or structured index. The system uses file names and chunk names as basic identifiers. When a file is uploaded:

The client sends the file name and size to the server.

The server generates chunk names sequentially (e.g., chunk_0, chunk_1).

There is no global lookup mechanism for reconstructing a file unless the chunk list is preserved manually.

While this approach simplifies the architecture, it also limits functionality such as file search, validation, and reassembly. It introduces potential challenges if the server is restarted or if peers are changed without updating the server's configuration.

3.5 Download Functionality (Planned)
The client GUI offers an interface for downloading a file, but the current implementation does not fully support file retrieval. The same function is invoked for both upload and download operations:

threading.Thread(target=send_file_to_main_server, args=(file_name,)).start()

While this allows the client to initiate a download request, the actual logic for retrieving chunks from peers and reassembling the file on the client side remains unimplemented. A future version of the system would involve:

Maintaining a mapping of chunks to peer nodes.

Sending retrieval requests to appropriate peers.

Collecting and merging chunks in sequence.
18/04/25, 11:35 - Sabhya: Implementation and Setup:
The implementation of the system is structured around three core modules: the central server, the peer nodes, and the GUI-based client. These components work in tandem to enable the upload, chunking, and distribution of files in a peer-to-peer network. The system is implemented entirely in Python using built-in libraries, ensuring ease of deployment and reducing dependencies. The architecture is modular and designed to simulate distributed computing concepts in a local network environment.

4.1 Technology Stack
The system uses a minimal and accessible technology stack to demonstrate core networking and concurrency concepts:

Programming Language: Python 3

Networking: Python socket (TCP-based)

Concurrency: Python threading module

Frontend GUI: tkinter (Python standard library)

File I/O: os, filedialog, messagebox

This stack was chosen to give direct control over communication logic, without abstraction layers such as REST or gRPC.

4.2 Deployment Environment
The system is designed for operation over a local area network (LAN). It has been tested on a set of three machines connected over the same network:

One machine runs the main server (server.py)

Two machines run independent peer servers (peer2.py)

One machine (can be the same as the server) runs the GUI client (peer1.py)

Each machine must know the IP addresses and ports of the others. These values are hardcoded in the server script:

PEERS = [ ('10.160.65.237', 9090), ('10.160.65.93', 9090), ]

4.3 Execution Procedure
To run the system, the following steps must be executed:

Start Peer Nodes
Each peer runs the file peer2.py, which starts a TCP server on port 9090. This server continuously listens for incoming chunk data and stores it in a folder named received_chunks.

Start the Main Server
The server is launched via server.py. It binds to IP 0.0.0.0 and port 8080 and waits for connections from clients. It handles upload requests by receiving a file, splitting it into 1 MB chunks, and sending these chunks to the peer nodes in a round-robin manner.

Launch the GUI Client
The GUI (peer1.py) provides two buttons: ‚ÄúUpload File‚Äù and ‚ÄúDownload File.‚Äù The upload process opens a file selection dialog and sends the file to the server.

File Upload Workflow

The client connects to the server on port 8080.

It sends a control byte to indicate the upload operation (client_socket.send(b'1')).

It sends the file name and a dummy peer IP (0.0.0.0), followed by the file size and content in chunks.

File Chunking and Distribution

The server writes the file to server_temp/, then splits it using a fixed chunk size (1 MB).

Chunks are named sequentially (chunk_0, chunk_1, ...) and stored in the chunks/ directory.

Each chunk is sent to a peer after a READY response is received.

Download Placeholder

The ‚ÄúDownload File‚Äù button in the GUI allows users to input a file name, but the download logic is currently the same as upload and requires further implementation.

4.4 Communication Protocol
All communication in the system occurs over TCP sockets. There is no use of high-level frameworks or APIs, which gives full control over the structure and order of data transmission.

4.4.1 Client-to-Server Communication
The client initiates a connection to the server using:

client_socket = socket.socket(socket.AF_INET, socket.SOCK_STREAM)
client_socket.connect((MAIN_SERVER_IP, MAIN_SERVER_PORT))

To indicate an upload, it sends:

client_socket.send(b'1')

It then sends:

File name (e.g., using file_path.encode())

Dummy peer IP (client_socket.send(dummy_peer_ip.encode()))

File size

File contents (in 1 MB reads)

The server receives the file, writes it to disk, and responds with:

client_socket.send(b" File received successfully.")

4.4.2 Server-to-Peer Communication
The server connects to each peer using a new socket for every chunk. The sequence is as follows:

Send chunk name:
s.send(os.path.basename(chunk_path).encode())

Wait for READY acknowledgment:
ack = s.recv(1024)

If READY, send the chunk file:
with open(chunk_path, 'rb') as f: while True: data = f.read(CHUNK_SIZE) if not data: break s.sendall(data)

Close the connection.

4.4.3 Peer Behavior
Peers operate in an infinite loop, accepting incoming connections:

server_socket = socket.socket(socket.AF_INET, socket.SOCK_STREAM)
server_socket.bind((HOST, PORT))
server_socket.listen(10)

Each peer performs:

Chunk name reception (chunk_name = conn.recv(1024).decode().strip())

Send READY (conn.send(b'READY'))

Receive binary chunk data and write it to received_chunks/

  for fileid in gutenberg.fileids():
    num_char = len(gutenberg.raw(fileid))
    num_words = len(gutenberg.words(fileid))
    num_sents = len(gutenberg.sents(fileid))
    avg_chars_per_word = num_char / num_words
    ttr = len(set(gutenberg.words(fileid))) / num_words

    print(f"Text: {fileid}")
    print(f"Total numbers of characters: {num_char}")
    print(f"Total number of words: {num_words}")
    print(f"Total number of sentences: {num_sents}")
    print(f"Average characters per word: {avg_chars_per_word: .2f}")
    print(f"Type-Token Ratio (TTR): {ttr: .2f}")
    print()
